<div id="review_2361636088" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2361636088" />
    <a title="Tatiana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana"><img alt="Tatiana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1249783659p2/2250020.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2361636088?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tatiana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tatiana" href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana">Tatiana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=locus">locus</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=nebula">nebula</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=hugo">hugo</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2361636088" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3824389576050845728">This was some dense, hard sci-fi. Dense like I remember <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/234225.Dune__Dune_Chronicles___1_' title='Dune (Dune Chronicles, #1) by Frank Herbert' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dune</a> being dense, when it had taken me 200 pages to get a grip on it. Same thing with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/26118426.Ninefox_Gambit__The_Machineries_of_Empire__1_' title='Ninefox Gambit (The Machineries of Empire #1) by Yoon Ha Lee' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ninefox Gambit</a>, and frankly I’d be lying if I said I knew what the whole calendar thing was all about. So I simply accepted it as something people fought over and moved on to the story. Which is essentially a Star Wars scenario with intergalactic battles and political schemes. All of this though was made interesting by the main characters. A young rising m</span>
  <span id="freeText3824389576050845728" style="display:none">This was some dense, hard sci-fi. Dense like I remember <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/234225.Dune__Dune_Chronicles___1_" title="Dune (Dune Chronicles, #1) by Frank Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Dune</a> being dense, when it had taken me 200 pages to get a grip on it. Same thing with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/26118426.Ninefox_Gambit__The_Machineries_of_Empire__1_" title="Ninefox Gambit (The Machineries of Empire #1) by Yoon Ha Lee" rel="nofollow noopener">Ninefox Gambit</a>, and frankly I’d be lying if I said I knew what the whole calendar thing was all about. So I simply accepted it as something people fought over and moved on to the story. Which is essentially a Star Wars scenario with intergalactic battles and political schemes. All of this though was made interesting by the main characters. A young rising military star and her ghostly mentor/mass murderer. They are a dynamic duo, and I was fascinated by their personal journeys and the way they worked together as a team. <br /><br />Yes, the book was totally incomprehensible at times, but also totally worth all the hard work.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1640293635" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1640293635" />
    <a title="Carly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4490040-carly"><img alt="Carly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1387424174p2/4490040.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1640293635?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carly" href="/user/show/4490040-carly">Carly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4490040-carly?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4490040-carly?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1640293635" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16467406667995346984">This book is <i>awesome.</i> And I mean that in the formal sense of the word: my mind is officially blown.<br/><br/>There is so much to <i>Ninefox Gambit</i> that it's hard to figure out where to start. The story takes place in a world governed by calendrical systems: in effect, the beliefs, calendar, and observances of society create topologies that in turn affect the laws of physics and allow the use of "exotic effects," which are almost always utilized as weapons. Cheris lives within the hexarchate, which is run by </span>
  <span id="freeText16467406667995346984" style="display:none">This book is <i>awesome.</i> And I mean that in the formal sense of the word: my mind is officially blown.<br /><br />There is so much to <i>Ninefox Gambit</i> that it's hard to figure out where to start. The story takes place in a world governed by calendrical systems: in effect, the beliefs, calendar, and observances of society create topologies that in turn affect the laws of physics and allow the use of "exotic effects," which are almost always utilized as weapons. Cheris lives within the hexarchate, which is run by six groups, each with their own distinct characteristics and symbol, from the burning suicide hawk of the Kel to the crafty Shuos ninefox. Cheris, a member of the straightforwardly warlike Kel, finds herself paired with the most infamous Shuos of all, on a mission to save the supposedly impregnable Fortress of Scattered Needles from calendrical heresy. All too quickly, she finds herself in a constant battle of wits with a formidable ally, constantly struggling to determine her true enemies.<br /> <br />One of the most brilliant aspect of the book is the way in which it places the reader within a world and a culture and a mindset so alien from our own. The hexarchate is not a pleasant place, constantly at war with all of its neighbors, ruthlessly destroying the cultures of all those it conquers, and scrupulously performing bloody "remembrance rights" to keep the calendrical systems strong. The Kel may be innately loyal, but to better fulfill their role as the hexarchate's disposable army, they are brainwashed and programmed with "formation instinct" so that they are effectively incapable of disobeying orders. Given the structure of the world, it's not surprising that the book is both dark and intense. From the perspective of the hexarchate's stultifying culture, the story tackles issues of gender and rape. Death is a constant throughout the story, and it is portrayed as brutal and grim rather than valiant and heroic. As one character thinks: <blockquote>"War is all about taking the future away from people."</blockquote><br />Despite the dark themes, I couldn't help but find the calendrical system just plain <i>awesome</i>. As one character puts it: <blockquote>"In a sense, all calendrical war is a game between competing sets of rules, fueled by the coherence of our beliefs. To win a calendrical war, you have to understand how game systems work."</blockquote> The concept of a sufficiently large population's faith, belief, and rituals actually warping the physical laws of the universe is just the <i>coolest thing ever.</i> Full disclosure: I have to admit that I suffer from Math Envy: while I don't think I'm really capable of understanding higher mathematics, I'm utterly fascinated by the core ideas, at least in the abstract. I dropped out of topology in the first few weeks because my brain felt like silly putty. I survived first-semester real analysis by the skin of my teeth and even so, I wandered around in a daze for weeks because <i>now I knew what real numbers <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cantor_set" rel="nofollow noopener">actually were.</a></i> This book was so fascinating that it gave me a newfound desire to try to learn topology again. <br /><br />But even apart from the mathematical aspects, the worldbuilding is fascinatingly deep and infinitely creative: disregarded self-aware artificial intelligences who act as servitors for the hexarchate, immortality devices and the black cradle, battle formation geometries, deadly threshold winnowers, human composite technologies, much else besides. And throughout the story is a constant theme of games, and games within games. As one opponent tells Cheris, <blockquote>"You lost the moment you agreed to play the game on my terms, without negotiating."</blockquote><i>Ninefox Gambit</i> is an impressively creative story brimming over with metaphor and symbolism and and analysis. If you're a fan of mathematics and mindgames, you really need to check out this book. I can't wait for the sequel.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>~~I received this ebook from the publisher, Solaris/Rebellion, in exchange for my honest review. Quotes are taken from an advanced reader copy and while they may not reflect the final phrasing, I believe they speak to the spirit of the novel as a whole.~~</b>
</i><br /><br /><i>Cross-posted on <a href="http://pagefault.booklikes.com/post/1418486/war-is-all-about-taking-the-future-away-from-people" rel="nofollow noopener">BookLikes.</a></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="16467406667995346984" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1685045433?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie" href="/user/show/1143969-julie">Julie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/29503206-ninefox-gambit">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=not-my-cup-of-tea">not-my-cup-of-tea</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=lgbtqa">lgbtqa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=space">space</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=dmla">dmla</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=hugo-awards-2017">hugo-awards-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=great-premise-lacking-execution">great-premise-lacking-execution</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1685045433" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer421483467137834774">2017 Hugo nominee for Best Novel -- and sigh, I have a lot of words. I know this book has gotten a lot of gushing 5-stars, so fair warning that I am not going to be as positive!<br/><br/>I already knew that I was on rocky terms with Lee's writing, having read a couple of his short stories and been ambivalent about one, and outright hated the other ("Combustion Hour" was literally the only story I wound up skipping in the 2014 Tor anthology). But I was hoping that with a longer pagecount and thus more spac</span>
  <span id="freeText421483467137834774" style="display:none">2017 Hugo nominee for Best Novel -- and sigh, I have a lot of words. I know this book has gotten a lot of gushing 5-stars, so fair warning that I am not going to be as positive!<br /><br />I already knew that I was on rocky terms with Lee's writing, having read a couple of his short stories and been ambivalent about one, and outright hated the other ("Combustion Hour" was literally the only story I wound up skipping in the 2014 Tor anthology). But I was hoping that with a longer pagecount and thus more space for him to set up his worldbuilding &amp; ideas, that it would make more sense.<br /><br />Not so.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1738746691" rel="nofollow noopener">Philip</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1962006444" rel="nofollow noopener">Ashley</a> have eloquently described the disorienting and alienating experience of reading this book; it genuinely feels like reading a foreign language due to how obtuse it is. I like to consider myself a pretty canny reader and have a quick grasp of new, unfamiliar terminology and technology/magic -- I've often said that I prefer when authors respect their readers by tossing them into the water and trusting them to catch on, not hand-holding with long dull exposition about "this is our world, and this is how it works".<br /><br />But the thing is, you gotta strike a balance. In books that successfully toss you in the deep end, you might take a little while to learn the terminology, but you're not left questioning the basic fundamentals of the world you're in. Whereas Lee <i>never</i> gives the necessary context for the most foundational things -- e.g. how in the world does the calendar change reality? how does calendrical rot work? how the hell is math used in battle? what is invariant ice and how does it work? what exactly is corpse-glass? for that matter, is the Fortress in space or floating in air? the sheer description was so vague that I never had a good mental image of <i>the place where all the action took place.</i> I didn't realise that there were even civilian homes and shops inside the Fortress until over halfway into the book!<br /><br />Of course you can handwave the window-dressing (I didn't care about the technicalities of the thresher winnower or amputation gun, for example), but your <i>world</i> has to make sense. It has to feel real. But this place never felt more than vaguely-sketched out to me. I kept reading because I hoped something would click, that by the end I'd have a fuller picture of how everything worked, but that never happened. Instead there are constant nonsensical word-dumps of mathematical terms that make it sound Very Smart, but it's just <i>gibberish</i>, impossible to follow and thus impossible to care about. This is billed as military SF, but I couldn't even be interested in the military clashes, because I couldn't at all visualise <i>what was even happening.</i> I can only imagine how exponentially more confusing this would've been as an audiobook.<br /><br />In the end the only way I forced myself through this was by resigning myself to the fact that I wasn't going to understand anything, and hanging on for the few segments that did interest me (and which is what earned the book its second star from me), namely:<br /><blockquote>• Cheris and Jedao's conversations, once they got away from tactics. I did like the concept of their collaboration and unique cooperation, and the gender-bending fluidity of their merged identity (and both their fluidity of sexuality, even).<br /><br />• The servitors! Let me just read an entire book about quiet little AIs scuttling around in the background with their secret parliaments tbh.<br /><br />• The epistolary letters between the heretics. As few as they were, these letters had so much more personality than Cheris herself.</blockquote><br />As hinted above, in addition to the worldbuilding bafflement, I have issues with the characters. Jedao seems more fleshed-out, but Cheris herself seems like a collection of traits more than a person. I can name you some things about her -- she likes romantic dramas, she's good at math but mediocre at dueling, she's kind to the servitors, and she's into women -- but that's all; I can't wax poetic about how she <i>behaves</i>, what she would do in a situation, what she believes and cares about, because I have no damn idea. She is literally a vessel to be possessed by Jedao. She's v. passive/reactive, only performing her duty and reacting to what's thrown at her, never taking a step to advance the plot, which I think is in large part why I didn't care about her.<br /><br />There are some interesting ideas here -- I genuinely liked the different factions of the Hexarchate, the personality differences between them, and I was intrigued by formation instinct -- but the execution is just not my cuppa.<br /><br />The last 10% was the strongest part of the book imo, but I also have problems with the ending.<br /> <b>Some spoilery discussion:</b> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I was finally riveted to the page once we got Jedao's backstory flashbacks, as you <i>finally</i> get some valuable information: character development, context for his rebellion and characterisation, a peeling-back of the curtain, showing someone with agency setting about a plan. I really enjoyed following his memories and thus understanding him better.<br /><br />But by the end of the book, the only real events of note that I can pinpoint are: 1) Kel Command killing Cheris' swarm in an attempt to get at Jedao, and 2) her ingesting the corpse-glass that means her becoming a more complete reincarnation of Jedao.<br /><br />But <i>that's it.</i> They haven't taken the Fortress. Jedao is still anchored to a mathematician, just as he was at the beginning of the book. He's still readying for his war against the Hexarchate. All that has happened of significance is learning his backstory. So it all just reads as a drawn-out prologue to the next book. I would've vastly preferred if this had <i>just</i> been the last 10%, if Jedao's backstory were a streamlined novelette setting up the next book. Goodreads tells me this was 384 pages, but it felt like 600. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <b>[/spoilers]</b><br /><br />In closing: In <a href="http://www.puffstheplay.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Puffs the Play</a> (a Harry Potter spoof that I love dearly and have seen 3 times), one of the characters is a mathematical savant excited to start MIT at age eleven, but he finds out he's a Muggleborn wizard and has to go to Hogwarts instead. Academics completely reset. He doesn't know any of the history, he can't cast spells, and keeps getting shunted into remedial magic classes. When he fails casting a spell for the nth time, he bursts into tears, sobbing "I'm a smart person! <i>I'm a smart person!</i>"<br /><br />That is how I felt, reading <u>Ninefox Gambit</u>. I'm a smart reader. I swear I'm a smart reader! And I know that others have loved this book a lot. So: let's just shelve this series under "not my cup of tea" and call it a day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="421483467137834774" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1831332528" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1831332528" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1831332528?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=distant-worlds">distant-worlds</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=military-sf">military-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=fantastic-weird">fantastic-weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=galaxy-spanning">galaxy-spanning</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=hard-sf">hard-sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1831332528" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5906004783180399265"><b>Ninefox Gambit: Careful or You’ll Catch Calendrical Rot</b><br/>Originally posted at <a href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com/reviews/ninefox-gambit/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Literature</a><br/>I’m just going to add my two cents here, as a heretic who refuses to conform to the calendrical hierarchy that forms the basis of this mathematical military hard SF space opera with some gender-bending thrown in for extra flavoring. <b>Ninefox Gambit</b> has drawn favorable comparisons to <b>Ada Palmer’s Too Like the Lighting</b>, because this book not only throws you off the deep end, but chains you up in neologisms</span>
  <span id="freeText5906004783180399265" style="display:none"><b>Ninefox Gambit: Careful or You’ll Catch Calendrical Rot</b><br />Originally posted at <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com/reviews/ninefox-gambit/" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a><br />I’m just going to add my two cents here, as a heretic who refuses to conform to the calendrical hierarchy that forms the basis of this mathematical military hard SF space opera with some gender-bending thrown in for extra flavoring. <b>Ninefox Gambit</b> has drawn favorable comparisons to <b>Ada Palmer’s Too Like the Lighting</b>, because this book not only throws you off the deep end, but chains you up in neologisms, a complex future society that is not gift-wrapped neatly with a bow, and then chucks you in the magma of an intergalactic battle between the Hexarchate and heretics who refuse to follow the consensus reality, which powers the exotic technology and weaponry with which this empire maintains its vice-grip on its subjects. As others have observed, replace high-level mathematical formulae with magical spells, and voila, you’ve got a space-fantasy novel.<br /><br />Of course <b>Ninefox Gambit</b> is far more complicated than that, but the basic story is of Kel soldier Charis teaming up with an immortal, homicidal, and totally insane undead general named Shuos Jedao to put down a heretical rebellion that has captured the supposedly-impregnable Fortress of Scattered Needles. Great names throughout, by the way, for that classical Three Kingdoms Chinese-fable feel but plugged into a hyper-militaristic future empire. You can react one of two ways — either your mind will rebel at the relentless stream of weird and confusing neologisms and mid-stream action opening sequence and say, “WTF was that?” Or … no that’s probably the standard response. The question then becomes, “Do I stick with it and hope that things will fall into place in time?” or “Do I ditch this book even though everyone is raving about how brilliant it is and I don’t want to be the loser who couldn’t handle the steep learning curve?”<br /><br />Well, one thing about audiobooks is that even if you’re in way over your head, unlike in the print version where eventually you just cannot carry on any further, unless you click stop the audiobook keeps playing. And since 11 hours isn’t really THAT long, once you’ve gone halfway it would be silly to give up, so just let those bizarre events flow past your ears, and pluck a couple words here and there and try to figure them out. I found the exchanges between Charis and Jedao to be the most interesting, and the exotic and muddled futuristic battle sequences and protected siege to be the most boring. I mean, who couldn’t love an insane undead homicidal general who doesn’t even act apologetic for killing a million people and shooting his own officers in cold blood? Now that’s a character that will stay in your memory for a while.<br /><br />Charis is a much more measured person, a soldier who is trying to do her duty, but knowing she is playing with a primal force that cannot be contained but is a “necessary evil,” that old chestnut. I found interesting echoes of <b>Ann Leckie’s Ancillary Justice</b>, particularly, in the subtle subversion of genders that are casually sprinkled at random moments. Yes, we’re pretty sure Charis is female and Jedao used to be male (he’s just a computer program now), and as audiobook narrator Emily Woo Zeller chooses to use “cigar-chomping drill sergeant” for every male military character, it’s clear from the voices who should be male and who female. But then out of nowhere sexual scenes don’t play out in the usual way, and there is the same obsession with gloves and the military found in <b>Ancillary Justice</b>. Is this a “thing” now? I hadn’t realized.<br /><br />In any case, I haven’t even bothered to describe the plot because a) it’s quite complicated, b) other reviewers have done that already, and c) I just don’t have the discipline this time. But suffice to say I was of two minds about <b>Ninefox Gambit</b>. One the one hand, I did like how Yoon Ha Lee just decided “screw it, I won’t explain anything – you do the work.” But that puts a lot of stress on the reader/listener, and if the events you are describing are not gripping (like <b>Dune</b>, for instance, equally baroque and complex but also a rip-roaring space opera extravaganza), then your attention is going to wander … like a heretic! So I found myself stopping and rewinding again and again, over and over, till I decided it wasn’t worth doing that anymore. And while I struggled to follow the storyline at many points, I appreciated the exotic world-building and mathematical magic, and the love-hate relationship of Charis and Jedao.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5906004783180399265" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer704692331997328350">This book is a wild, hallucinogenic space adventure set in a far-future society that manages to be alien while retaining the memory of how fragile and beautiful individualistic humanity can be. There are Brave Robot Pals and a complicated friendship (...can you call it a friendship? well, I would, after everything) between a dude and a lady at the center of the story that manages to be one of the most horrifying/fascinating partnerships I've ever read. SIGN ME UP FOR THE SEQUEL even though I'm s</span>
  <span id="freeText704692331997328350" style="display:none">This book is a wild, hallucinogenic space adventure set in a far-future society that manages to be alien while retaining the memory of how fragile and beautiful individualistic humanity can be. There are Brave Robot Pals and a complicated friendship (...can you call it a friendship? well, I would, after everything) between a dude and a lady at the center of the story that manages to be one of the most horrifying/fascinating partnerships I've ever read. SIGN ME UP FOR THE SEQUEL even though I'm still not sure WTF calendrical rot is. Don't judge me, okay, I'm new to these types of books.<br /><br />My B&amp;N review: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.barnesandnoble.com/blog/sci-fi-fantasy/ninefox-gambit-is-a-space-opera-to-tax-your-brain-and-ignite-your-imagination/">www.barnesandnoble.com/blog/sci-fi-fa...</a><br />Lady Business Review: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://ladybusiness.dreamwidth.org/2016/06/20/lets-get-literate-dont-trust-a-fox-unless-its-a-robot-fox.html">https://ladybusiness.dreamwidth.org/2...</a><br />Fangirl Happy Hour discussion: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.fangirlhappyhour.com/2016/07/24/fangirl-happy-hour-episode-49-nostalgia-fail/">http://www.fangirlhappyhour.com/2016/...</a></span>

<div id="review_2068246213" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2068246213" />
    <a title="Meagan ✊&amp;#x1f3fc; Blacklivesmatter ✊&amp;#x1f3fc;Blacktranslivesmatter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27847867-meagan-x1f3fc-blacklivesmatter-x1f3fc-blacktranslivesmatter"><img alt="Meagan ✊&amp;#x1f3fc; Blacklivesmatter ✊&amp;#x1f3fc;Blacktranslivesmatter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572058255p2/27847867.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2068246213?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meagan ✊&amp;#x1f3fc; Blacklivesmatter ✊&amp;#x1f3fc;Blacktranslivesmatter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meagan ✊&amp;#x1f3fc; Blacklivesmatter ✊&amp;#x1f3fc;Blacktranslivesmatter" href="/user/show/27847867-meagan-x1f3fc-blacklivesmatter-x1f3fc-blacktranslivesmatter">Meagan ✊&amp;#x1f3fc; Blacklivesmatter ✊&amp;#x1f3fc;Blacktranslivesmatter</a>
      </span>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer2075836282114720760"><i>"I'm your gun"</i><br/><br/>This was even more amazing the second time around. Mostly because I understood so much more 😜. I can't wait to finish the rest of the series. <br/><br/>Another reviewer mentioned the concept of <i>consensus reality</i> which is the shared beliefs of the majority that shape the believers perception of reality. Interesting concept and it is interesting to Lee's interpretation of that play out in this book. The first time around I hadn't even caught on to the whole "beliefs shape reality" aspect of th</span>
  <span id="freeText2075836282114720760" style="display:none"><i>"I'm your gun"</i><br /><br />This was even more amazing the second time around. Mostly because I understood so much more 😜. I can't wait to finish the rest of the series. <br /><br />Another reviewer mentioned the concept of <i>consensus reality</i> which is the shared beliefs of the majority that shape the believers perception of reality. Interesting concept and it is interesting to Lee's interpretation of that play out in this book. The first time around I hadn't even caught on to the whole "beliefs shape reality" aspect of the book (I missed so much!)<br /><br />I liked the set up of the narrative and the minimum exposition. It was so much more rewarding when I figured out an aspect of the world on my own with the minimal clues the author provided.<br />______________________________________________<br />2017 review<br />Lee had a way of warping traditional concepts like gender, society, government, weapons tech, time, human perception and transgenderism that made you struggle to wrap your head around it initially, but that forced you to think outside the box. Lee subtley provides prespective on some controversial topics like homosexulaity, polyamory and transgenderism, but never tells you what to think or outright states an opinion, nonetheless it is there for you to ponder. I absolutely loved that about this book. I will admit that Lee just drops you into the middle of the world of Ninefox Gambit and leaves you to your own devices as far as understanding and interpreting the world. At first I really struggled with the out of the box concepts, I almost stopped reading once or twice, but I was determined to figure this world out. I just knew if I held on for a little longer things would start to click into place. Oh I was not disappointed! <br /><br />I went into this thinking it was going to be your standard military sci-fi/space opera. I think that is what really made me struggle even more. I kept approaching every concept in the wrong way. Once I figured out that this book actually contains a magic system based on the intersections of science and math and how that in turn shapes the physical world, I was much better off. This book is definitely science fantasy. With that said it still had all the elements I enjoy to read in military sci-fi/space opera. It had the amazing, and very unique, weapons tech, political turmoil, military maneuvers/operations, characters questioning if they truly are on the right side and revolution among other things. <br /><br />The only negative I have is that things dragged a little during the middle. This is because battle wasn't your typical "shoot 'em up" and "bombs away". So battles were much more focused on the military strategy and psychological warfare as well. BUT the weapons were still EPIC!<br /><br />This was such a quirky and novel read. I ABSOLUTELY LOVED it. I will definitely continue this series!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2075836282114720760" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1978493180" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1978493180" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36819318-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485843897p2/36819318.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1978493180?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/36819318-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36819318-scott?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1978493180" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3967212005669369961"><i>Ninefox Gambit</i> begins with a desperate battle.<br/><br/>I’m not talking about the battle that Kel Cheris fights in the first chapter of the story. That’s a pretty serious fight, but it pales next to the backs-to-the wall combat that you, the reader, will face. Prepare for full scale bombardment with a vast arsenal of factions, the screaming howl of incoming volleys of neologisms and massed assaults of odd and confusing names supported by an armored division of incomprehensible techno-magic. I only just ma</span>
  <span id="freeText3967212005669369961" style="display:none"><i>Ninefox Gambit</i> begins with a desperate battle.<br /><br />I’m not talking about the battle that Kel Cheris fights in the first chapter of the story. That’s a pretty serious fight, but it pales next to the backs-to-the wall combat that you, the reader, will face. Prepare for full scale bombardment with a vast arsenal of factions, the screaming howl of incoming volleys of neologisms and massed assaults of odd and confusing names supported by an armored division of incomprehensible techno-magic. I only just made it through – I nearly threw down my arms and raised the white flag twenty pages in – and I’m glad I persevered. Lee’s novel is well worth the initial struggle. <br /><br />The story focuses on Cheris, who lives in a far future interstellar civilisation that is run by six factions- The Hexarchate. Cheris’ faction is the suicidal, charge-into-battle warrior segment of society, and she is a talented, if unorthodox soldier in a society where being unorthodox gets you killed.<br /><br />OK, that’s the easy stuff. Beyond this, the Hexarchate runs on technology that relies on a shared calendar- it is temporally dependent, and requires a consistent consensus on the calendar from its subjects in order to function. When the calendar is working as it should the forces of the Hexarchy can create weapons by forming certain military formations, and they have a vast arsenal of strange weapons (fungus bombs, bombs that turn people into crystalized memories, etc.) they can deploy, all of which are underpinned by arcane, savant-level calendrical math. If the calendar is altered -something known as ‘calendrical heresy’ - then many weapons no longer work or are unpredictable. <br /><br />This is sold as tech, but really, it’s complicated magic way beyond the ken of the average person, much like the magic in Lev Grossman's <i>The Magicians</i>. You’ll need to suspend your disbelief a bit while reading <i>Ninefox Gambit</i> as there isn't a great deal of science in here. One particular weapon- A ‘Threshold Winnower’ was discussed twice (and used, too) but I still had no real idea how it worked or what it really did, other than weirdly kill everyone around it, like some sort of d&amp;d magic spell. <br /><br />Anyway, some heretics take over one of the Hexarchates most important, near impenetrable fortresses, spreading their corruption out into the rest of the Hexarchy. Cheris is roped into leading the assault on the stronghold, and the spirit/ghost of Shuos Jedao, the hexarchates most legendary general, a man who is also the civilisation’s worst traitor and a mass murderer of his own men, is loaded into her brain to advise her. With Jedao in her head Cheris begins her assault, and begins a journey where she discovers a great deal about the darker side of her world, and the man she is now sharing her body with.<br /><br /><i>Ninefox Gambit</i> is a damned entertaining novel full of interesting ideas. Just don’t expect your hand to be held (at <i>all</i>) or for anything much to be explained. I firmly believe that good SF should make the reader work a bit, and that this effort can be rewarding, but Lee isn’t spotting you while you’re on the bench press- he’s piling up the weights, waiting until you’re straining to lift them, and then ducking out for a coffee. Anyway, once I got used to the paucity of explanation and just rolled with things I really enjoyed this novel.<br /><br />The story races along. The weird ‘tech’, Cheris’ characterisation, her strange and cautious relationship with the genius mass murderer who she shares her head with, and the slow reveal of the history of their civilisation all add up to a really interesting story. Jedao himself is also a genuinely interesting character, whose motives are never clear, and whose history is dark, bloody and surprising. <br /><br />I got totally sucked in to all of this, and I raced through this book, enjoying it much more than I thought I would. I’m looking forward to the sequel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3967212005669369961" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1485761661" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1485761661" />
    <a title="Tudor Ciocarlie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4854547-tudor-ciocarlie"><img alt="Tudor Ciocarlie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301936603p2/4854547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1485761661?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tudor Ciocarlie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tudor Ciocarlie" href="/user/show/4854547-tudor-ciocarlie">Tudor Ciocarlie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4854547-tudor-ciocarlie?shelf=2016-my-best-of">2016-my-best-of</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4854547-tudor-ciocarlie?shelf=sf-series">sf-series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1485761661" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8775368762185149676">Ninefox Gambit is the best novel of 2016 and exactly my kind of science-fiction. For me, the most important quality of a speculative fiction book is its "cognitive estrangement" (as China Mieville calls it), the degree to which the fiction takes you beyond your known world and throws you out of your comfort zone. And the estrangement can't get more alien than the world of Ninefox Gambit, an immersive and unforgettable universe in which the galactic government - the Hexarchate, formed by six very</span>
  <span id="freeText8775368762185149676" style="display:none">Ninefox Gambit is the best novel of 2016 and exactly my kind of science-fiction. For me, the most important quality of a speculative fiction book is its "cognitive estrangement" (as China Mieville calls it), the degree to which the fiction takes you beyond your known world and throws you out of your comfort zone. And the estrangement can't get more alien than the world of Ninefox Gambit, an immersive and unforgettable universe in which the galactic government - the Hexarchate, formed by six very different types of human beings, controls its people using a complex calendrical mathematical system, that forms and defines the reality in which the people live. The Hexarchate fights against "calendrical rot", that is used by groups of "heretics" to turn the nature of technology and the reality around them to their advantage. And not only the world is perfect in its estrangement, but also the brilliant characters, the rich prose and the complex structure. Ninefox Gambit is an extremely complex, confusing and alien novel, but for me these are its strongest qualities and exactly the aspects that make it an amazing read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8775368762185149676" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2233962679" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2233962679" />
    <a title="Trike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2135825-trike"><img alt="Trike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525980079p2/2135825.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2233962679?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trike" href="/user/show/2135825-trike">Trike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2135825-trike?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2135825-trike?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2135825-trike?shelf=sff-bookclub-challenge-2017">sff-bookclub-challenge-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2135825-trike?shelf=read-in-2017">read-in-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2135825-trike?shelf=sffbc-read">sffbc-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2233962679" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16628991047966452313">That was... weird.<br/><br/>I kinda think Lee knows that most Space Opera is just Fantasy in Space, so he went with that full force. (I must apologize because I don’t know if the author’s surname is Yoon or Lee. I’m going with Lee.) The story is pretty basic Space Opera: a bunch of rebels have secured a massive space station and the empire must root them out. Or, if you like, terrorists and armed forces. There’s no clear delineation between good guys and bad guys here, though, so you can look at it either</span>
  <span id="freeText16628991047966452313" style="display:none">That was... weird.<br /><br />I kinda think Lee knows that most Space Opera is just Fantasy in Space, so he went with that full force. (I must apologize because I don’t know if the author’s surname is Yoon or Lee. I’m going with Lee.) The story is pretty basic Space Opera: a bunch of rebels have secured a massive space station and the empire must root them out. Or, if you like, terrorists and armed forces. There’s no clear delineation between good guys and bad guys here, though, so you can look at it either way you want to. In that sense, this story is superbly ambiguous.<br /><br />The worldbuilding is also as interesting as any I’ve come across recently. The book this is most similar to is Ann Leckie’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17333324.Ancillary_Justice__Imperial_Radch___1_" title="Ancillary Justice (Imperial Radch, #1) by Ann Leckie" rel="nofollow noopener">Ancillary Justice</a>, and I had a similar reaction to this book, in that I can see why everyone likes it and I appreciate the chances it takes, but I didn’t really enjoy it. At the end of Leckie’s novel everything snaps into place and you realize why the preceding story had to be “just so”. Here, though, the ending was obvious from build-up. <br /><br />I think the reason for that is because this story is so extremely peculiar in its worldbbuilding and the concepts are so bizarre that I was able to focus on the key character elements as the only familiar touchstones, which made the conclusion inevitable.<br /><br />In this universe Lee has constructed, the rules of reality appear to be governed by mutual consent, taking the idea of “consensual reality” to its logical conclusion. If everyone believes in a thing, then that thing exists. The rebels — called “calendrical heretics” — are attempting to substitute their own reality. In this case, a form of representational democracy to oppose the existing oligarchy. The calendar thing is part of what maintains the status quo, apparently. Since everyone celebrates the same holidays and special events, it binds everyone together in a common belief system. It’s a common outlook on life made manifest by virtue of shared history.<br /><br />But by using such odd terms like “calendrical heretics” and “carrion shards” and the like, such as exotic weapons that amputate random body parts because the soldiers believe they will, it makes the interaction between the two main characters the only familiar ground to stand on. Which in turn led me to suss out the ending somewhere around the halfway point. I think this probably would have been more effective as a short story or novella, so the punchline didn’t have such a long build-up.<br /><br />I like the idea of the two main characters, too, with one a soldier who has disappointed her family by joining the armed forces and the other being the mental echo of a long-dead general who was put on ice 397 years earlier for winning war by essentially murdering his own army. The soldier is a highly-successful field commander who is invited to offer a proposal on how to defeat the rebels, and instead of proposing a strategy or exotic weapon, she instead says, “Let’s get the brilliant but insane general to advise us.” So they implant the general’s psyche into her brain as an advisor and send them off to deal with the heretics.<br /><br />Bits and pieces of this sound like things taken from other books, such as Dune and Starship Troopers and Ender’s Game and a book whose name I forget that uses the “voice of another person in the main character’s head” thing, but it ends up being its own story. <br /><br />I think that some people like this story because it makes them feel smart for figuring it out. Lee doesn’t hold your hand at all, and there are precious few infodumps so common in sci-fi. We just jump straight into the weirdness with words like cindermoths and ashhawks and calendrical heresies. Enough small clues are scattered throughout that you eventually find your footing, so I suspect that’s why people like it, but to me all of that just felt like an unnecessary distraction from the core story, which was actually a straightforward tale of not accepting the status quo just because you are told to.<br /><br />For me that payoff wasn’t worth the journey, but I clearly appear to be an outlier in that regard. YMMV.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16628991047966452313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1743866067" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1743866067" />
    <a title="Mimi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2490110-mimi"><img alt="Mimi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1424631975p2/2490110.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1743866067?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mimi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mimi" href="/user/show/2490110-mimi">Mimi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/29503206-ninefox-gambit">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mimi by: </span>
      S&amp;L January 2019
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2490110-mimi?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2490110-mimi?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2490110-mimi?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2490110-mimi?shelf=my-kindle">my-kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2490110-mimi?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2490110-mimi?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2490110-mimi?shelf=military-sci-fi">military-sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1743866067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8166833908298616355">A frustrating read that eventually came together in the end in a fairly spectacular way. Or maybe I'm just saying that because I've been fighting with this book for over a week now, and the euphoria of reaching the end to find answers waiting for me is temporarily blanketing most of my initial frustration. Whatever it is, I no longer resent the amount of work I had to put in to get to those answers, although your mileage may vary.<br/><br/>This novel is the very definition of "your mileage may vary" becau</span>
  <span id="freeText8166833908298616355" style="display:none">A frustrating read that eventually came together in the end in a fairly spectacular way. Or maybe I'm just saying that because I've been fighting with this book for over a week now, and the euphoria of reaching the end to find answers waiting for me is temporarily blanketing most of my initial frustration. Whatever it is, I no longer resent the amount of work I had to put in to get to those answers, although your mileage may vary.<br /><br />This novel is the very definition of "your mileage may vary" because it's hard to gauge, and I cannot think of one person I know, either online or irl, whom I'm certain would like it. That wouldn't stop me from recommending it though, if only so I'd get to see someone else go through what I did... hee hee.<br /><br />So what is this book about? I'm actually still trying to figure that out myself, and I've only gotten some of the pieces to fit in a way that makes sense. But let's give it a shot anyway.<br /><br /><strong>The basic plot is this:</strong> after leading an unsuccessful mission, young, talented, and extremely loyal Captain Kel Cheris gets another chance to save her career.<br /><blockquote>In a way each battle was home: a wretched home, where small mistakes went unnoticed, but a home nonetheless. She didn't know what it said about her that her duty suited her so well, but so long as it was her duty, it didn't matter what she thought about it.</blockquote><br />Cheris, along with a few of her peers, are recruited to a risky, high-profile assignment in which they each offer a new solution to a constant problem--a tricky faction rising up on one of the empire's planets. Cheris has an outrageous idea and it gets chosen almost immediately; she is too young and naive to give this much thought. The idea is to resurrect a dead general who never lost a battle, but who was also known for going mad and massacring a rebellion along with his own troops, to help her put down the current rebellion. So the spirit of dead, psychotic General Shuos Jedao is brought back to life as a consciousness that only Cheris can interact with. (He's called "undead" in the book's blurb, and that's just misleading, especially for people who read a lot of paranormal or urban fantasy...)<br /><br /><strong>The rest of the book</strong> is about Cheris and Jedao dealing with rebel forces all the while butting heads, battling over tactics, playing mind games, making hard decisions, and ultimately bonding. Literally.<br /><br />This is the first time in Cheris' life leading an operation of this size and caliber with the empire's backing, and there are plenty of tense moments and close calls for her throughout the book.<br /><br />Jedao himself is an enigma full of contradictions, and through him, Cheris begins to doubt, and later on, to realize that maybe she is fighting for the wrong side.<br /><br />We do get to learn what happened to Jedao all those years ago that led to the massacre. A short but poignant end to this part of the trilogy.<br /><br /><strong>None of this</strong> will make much sense though because you're thrown into the deep end in the very first scene and things only get more confusing from there. The writing is full of jargon and offers very few explanations, so you just have to roll with it. But with repeated exposure to these terms, you get used to them. Or not--your mileage may vary.<br /><br />There's a lot of talk about calendars and mathematical equations, which might lead you to think there's some kind of system or logic behind the tech, but there's really not. The tech here is akin to an elaborate magic system without all the elaborate explanations.<br /><br />Calendars are important because they are. Math makes this universe go round because it does. Heretics are those who dare to defy the empire by creating their own calendars to live by. New calendars are believed to weaken the empire's hold on power and unbalance the universe somehow. Therefore the heretics must be put down immediately. No exceptions. No mercy. <br /><blockquote>"I'm not complaining about the guns," [Cheris] said, "but guns change minds, not hearts. And calendrical rot is a matter of hearts."<br /><br />"It depends on what you shoot," Jedao said dryly.</blockquote><br />Which leads right into my next point: there is a high body count, as you'd expect with military fiction. And like good Mil-SF, the writing shows the mental and emotional toll the amount of killing and the methods of mass killing take on the people with their boots on the ground. In contrast, you get to see and compare that to those back on their home worlds plotting ways in which to use the death toll to further their own political careers. Hundreds of years in the future, and yet not much has changed on this front.<br /><blockquote>It was important to acknowledge numbers, especially when the dead were dead by your doing.</blockquote><br />A big part of my frustration with this book was sympathizing with the heretics/rebel forces while having to read the story from the point of view of the leader of an invading swarm. It pushed all the right buttons to get my heckles up, but I was too busy fighting the frustration to realize what was happening or that I was being played.<br /><br />I have a feeling I will appreciate this book more once I get through the whole trilogy. It's the kind of story that has the potential to stay with me for years to come, as I can already feel it knocking around in my head.<br /><br /><br />Cross-posted at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://covers2covers.wordpress.com/2019/01/08/ninefox-gambit-the-machineries-of-empire-1-by-yoon-ha-lee/">https://covers2covers.wordpress.com/2...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="8166833908298616355" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_1743866067'><a id="like_count_review_1743866067" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1743866067?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">23 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F26118426-ninefox-gambit">Like</a></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1828799429" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1828799429" />
    <a title="stormin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3809399-stormin"><img alt="stormin" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1828799429?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="stormin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="stormin" href="/user/show/3809399-stormin">stormin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3809399-stormin?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1828799429" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11097448352439359503">I read this book because it was billed as "Hard SF space opera" by a <a href='http://www.barnesandnoble.com/blog/sci-fi-fantasy/the-best-new-science-fiction-fantasy-books-of-the-year-so-far/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Barnes & Noble list</a> of the best sci-fi of 2016. <br/><br/>I've been reading science fiction for basically my entire life, but even for me hard SF can be pretty impenetrable, and more often than not I find that even the more ambitious and impressive works leave me cold and alienated. Take <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22816087.Seveneves' title='Seveneves by Neal Stephenson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Seveneves</a> as a recent example. But when it works, it <em>really</em> works. One of my favorite sci-fi books of all time is another <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson' title='Neal Stephenson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Neal Stephenson</a> book with a ha</span>
  <span id="freeText11097448352439359503" style="display:none">I read this book because it was billed as "Hard SF space opera" by a <a href="http://www.barnesandnoble.com/blog/sci-fi-fantasy/the-best-new-science-fiction-fantasy-books-of-the-year-so-far/" rel="nofollow noopener">Barnes &amp; Noble list</a> of the best sci-fi of 2016. <br /><br />I've been reading science fiction for basically my entire life, but even for me hard SF can be pretty impenetrable, and more often than not I find that even the more ambitious and impressive works leave me cold and alienated. Take <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22816087.Seveneves" title="Seveneves by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Seveneves</a> as a recent example. But when it works, it <em>really</em> works. One of my favorite sci-fi books of all time is another <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/545.Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Neal Stephenson</a> book with a hard SF pedigree: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2845024.Anathem" title="Anathem by Neal Stephenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Anathem</a>. The late <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8282782.Ian_M__Banks" title="Ian M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Ian M. Banks</a> is another writer who could really move me some of the time (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12009.The_Algebraist" title="The Algebraist by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">The Algebraist</a>) and also write books so off-putting that I never managed to get past the first few chapters (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/886066.Matter" title="Matter by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Matter</a>).<br /><br />All of this is a little bit irrelevant, however, because if you were to make a spectrum of "hard SF" on one end to "space magic" on the other end, Ninefox Gambit would be about as far on the "space magic"-end of that spectrum as it is possible to get. In fact, I'd say one of the strongest influences on the book is the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warhammer_40,000" rel="nofollow noopener">Warhammer 40,000</a> universe. While there are no orcs or elves in Ninefox Gambit, the technology and combat have absolutely zero to do with any recognizably feasible principles of physics. Throw in the crypto-religious references and the totalitarian theocratic nature of the principle empire, and the similarities really start to stack up.<br /><br />So there's absolutely nothing hard SF about this, but I can kind of see how a reviewer without a really firm grasp of sci-fi could get confused, because the book is as stuffed to the gills with dense, impenetrable, made-up vocabulary as (for a lot of non-specialists) hard SF can be. So, Ninefox Gambit is not hard SF, but it is as offputting and inaccessible as quite a lot of hard SF can be, so the confusion is forgivable. <br /><br />Don't get me wrong: this is a personal preference thing. Some people <em>like</em> exoticism more than they like relatability, and for folks like that this might really work. And I will say that--a day or two after putting the book down--I have an absolutely concrete, unwavering appreciation for the Kel and their ethic of loyalty. The Kel are one of the 6 major made-up factions in the book, and for them to leave such a clear and strong impression is really, well, impressive. In addition, the crazy space magic is actually really, really cool at times. The names for the weapons and ships are really compelling. All the space ships are "moths" for example, so you've got "box moths" (transport ships) and "needle moths" (fighters) and a few "cinder moths" (dreadnaughts, of which the empire only has a a few). In isolation that doesn't sound that neat, but in the context of all the other ship names, ship model names, weapon names, and so forth the overall effect is very cool. There are lightsaber-esque dueling swords, and infantry combat is all about applying cool-sounding (but utterly meaningless) formations that somehow confer protection against magical weapons, depending on the level of orthodoxy or heresy in the vicinity. (Hey: I told you it was space magic and not hard sf.)<br /><br />On a quick side note: for a while, I kept trying to read it as hard SF and coming up with ways to make sense of the magic. And if you had conditioned people (through years of genetic manipulation and brain control) to <em>believe</em> that they could be killed / injured based on inscrutable laws governing the intersection of orthodoxy/heresy and formation drilling, maybe that would make sense, but since the weapons in this book do things like turn people inside-out (literally) or turn them to glass figurines in the shape of their body but displaying their most private memories, I had to let that theory go.<br /><br />The book reminds me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17333324.Ancillary_Justice__Imperial_Radch___1_" title="Ancillary Justice (Imperial Radch, #1) by Ann Leckie" rel="nofollow noopener">Ancillary Justice</a> more than anything else. I don't think it's as well-crafted from the perspective of story structure. There's a massive and thinly-veiled info dump at the very end that is basically completely irrelevant to the story so far, and yet sets up the sequel in such a way that it makes this entire book itself <em>also</em> irrelevant. <br /><br />The infodump is irrelevant because it reveals that the reason for the mythical traitor general to have killed a million people (including hundreds of thousands of his own fanatically loyal troops) is (1) utterly unsatisfactory on any typical basis of human morality and (2) <em>exactly</em> as cliched as you were afraid it was. I mean, really, how many books do people have to write that follow the formula of "I'm going to write the most despicable character you can possibly imagine and have him commit every conceivable atrocity, but then justify it by making the evil empire he is fighting <em>even worse</em>." Uh, no thanks. A redemptive arc is one thing; at a certain point you bottom out, lose the arc, and are just plain wallowing. I don't have any desire to read that book once. I certainly don't have any desire to read it again and again and again. (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17333324.Ancillary_Justice__Imperial_Radch___1_" title="Ancillary Justice (Imperial Radch, #1) by Ann Leckie" rel="nofollow noopener">Ancillary Justice</a> isn't that bad, because the main character ends up as a kind of enlightened first-world poverty tourist, solving the problems of deprived underclasses wherever she goes. Instead, a book like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19161852.The_Fifth_Season__The_Broken_Earth___1_" title="The Fifth Season (The Broken Earth, #1) by N.K. Jemisin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fifth Season</a> or the entire <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deathstalker_(series)" rel="nofollow noopener">Deathstalker series</a> or maybe an given Warhammer 40k tie-in novel will provide other examples of this genre. There's more, too. If you took every book of this type that I gave up on in disgust, you'd probably be able to fill at least one shelf.<br /><br />Oh, and the infodump renders the entire book you just read pretty much irrelevant by basically overwriting the protagonist. She was pretty short on discernible personality for starters (and basically everyone else has even less), but by the time the infodump is done you can kiss what little empathy you've developed for her goodbye, because she's no longer the same person. Incidentally, this is the exact same problem that pretty much doomed Joss Whedon's <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dollhouse_(TV_series)" rel="nofollow noopener">Dollhouse series</a> from the start. I don't know why this is rocket science: but if you literally overwrite the personality of your protagonist in every episode then (newsflash!) you don't actually have a protagonist. You have a protagonist-shaped holed instead. In fairness, Whedon (or whoever was actually writing the story) figured that out in season 2, but it was too late to save the show. I'm actually going to give Yoon Ha Lee the benefit of the doubt and assume that--starting in the second book--the protagonist will remain stable, but that just renders the actual book that I actually just finished reading one of those long, superfluous prologues that so many fantasy writers can't seem to stop themselves from publishing at the start of their books.<br /><br />Oh, but I mentioned <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17333324.Ancillary_Justice__Imperial_Radch___1_" title="Ancillary Justice (Imperial Radch, #1) by Ann Leckie" rel="nofollow noopener">Ancillary Justice</a>. There are all kinds of similarities. For starters, there's the setup: an overtly theocratic, totalitarian empire on the verge of civil war. In both cases, the theology of said theocracy is also fairly exotic. In Ancillary Justice it seems to borrow from Eastern traditions and/or historical (especially: Roman) religious history. In Ninefox Gambit the actually content of the theology seems utterly irrelevant other than that it is viciously evil. Space travel, for example, depends on the population rigorously observing periodic public holidays in which criminals and heretics are publicly tortured to death. The point is that both Ancillary Justice and Ninefox Gambit present religious empires that are evil, byzantine, and ripe for civil war.<br /><br />There are plenty of other similarities as well. One is the role of gloves and bare hands as a social taboo. This is an odd focal point in both books, although when you're trying to make fictional taboos that are gender neutral for contemporary readers there aren't that many options available, I suppose. (Really, though, why not try something different? Say: eyes. What if everyone always worse opaque glasses in public, and actually looking someone in the eye is unspeakably intimate. That seems equally gender-neutral, but also much more interesting in the sense that eye contact really <em>is</em> deeply meaningful to human nature at a deep level. Oh well, nobody asked me.) The sci-fi tropes are also pretty similar: the technology is all at the "sufficiently advanced to be magic" level, there's a lot of emphasis on the identity-body relationship, and instead of aliens there are questions about the alien nature of human-created AI. (Well, there actually are aliens Ancillary Justice, and there might be in Ninefox Gambit, but they are initially peripheral in both cases.)<br /><br />So: time to wrap up. I was impressed by how immersive the setting was, and I do have a soft-spot for both military sci-fi and also for space magic. There were pretty serious structural flaws, however, and both the moral nihilism and the obsession with trying to make fratricidal psychopaths sympathetic is tiresome. I think the book will gather some real fans, but I'm not among their number.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11097448352439359503" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1546676444" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1546676444" />
    <a title="Gary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30706026-gary"><img alt="Gary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1465615148p2/30706026.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1546676444?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary" href="/user/show/30706026-gary">Gary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1546676444" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6637098382716677854">Ninefox Gambit is Yoon Ha Lee's first novel, though longtime readers of his amazing short fiction have been anticipating its advent for some time now. Ostensibly science fiction, as most military space-based adventure stories are, Ninefox Gambit highlights what Lee does best - science fantasy that feels like hard sci-fi. Lee's prose style has such perfect pitch and balance, and an effortless ability to convey a tone of ordinariness to even the most fantastical and absurd of his imaginings, and a</span>
  <span id="freeText6637098382716677854" style="display:none">Ninefox Gambit is Yoon Ha Lee's first novel, though longtime readers of his amazing short fiction have been anticipating its advent for some time now. Ostensibly science fiction, as most military space-based adventure stories are, Ninefox Gambit highlights what Lee does best - science fantasy that feels like hard sci-fi. Lee's prose style has such perfect pitch and balance, and an effortless ability to convey a tone of ordinariness to even the most fantastical and absurd of his imaginings, and a sense of humor as dry as dust and dark as the abyss. No one sells the irrational as rational like Yoon Ha Lee.<br /><br />The setting and plot of Ninefox Gambit are as perfectly tailored to his strengths as a writer as any of his short works. The Hexarchate is a fascist empire that explifies blurry rationale; unequivocally certain of it's own perfection, despite the fact that perfection entails using narcotics to induce loyalty in its subjects and engaging in perpetual warfare against an ever multiplying roster of "heretics". These heretics attempt to break free of the Hexarchate by subverting its High Calendar - an unbroken broadcast of the Hexarchate's "perfect" belief system, which also powers its "exotic" weaponry. Captain Kel Cheris is eyed with suspicion by her superiors because of her ability to adapt to heretical changes in the calendar and use them against the heretics. It also makes her the perfect candidate to retake the Fortress of Scattered Needles, a strategically important base whose inhabitants have engaged in a particularly dangerous form of "calendrical rot". Cheris' strategy for retaking the fortress is to merge with the undead General Shuos Jedao: a brilliant strategist who never lost a battle, but also went insane and murdered his own troops alongside the heretics. Jedao lives up to his reputation once Cheris' assault on the fortress begins, but his subtle mind games force her to question whether he is leading her down the same path to madness he once succumbed to, or worse yet the nagging suspicion that his actions weren't mad at all.<br /><br />There are too many things I loved about this novel to list them all. On the macro scale, its brilliant set pieces and compelling characters, careful plotting and use of dramatic irony to convey the constantly crossed purposes and contradictory goals of the Hexarchate and its subjects, and of course Lee's hypnotic, radiant prose. But as with most great works of fiction, it's the little touches that elevate this novel - the minor heresies everyone has to commit to get through their day with their sanity intact. Like the Lieutenant who is composing music in her head when she receives her orders, and laments that the "one problem with military life is that you can't schedule the interruptions." Tragically, she is unable to fully appreciate the ironies implicit in that statement, as the reader undoubtedly does.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6637098382716677854" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
